</ul>
<p>In addition, several other actors have filled more than one minor role on the series. <a href="/wiki/Kim_Strauss" title="Kim Strauss">Kim Strauss</a> played the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drazi" title="Drazi">Drazi Ambassador</a> in four episodes, as well as nine other characters in ten more episodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Some actors had difficulty dealing with the application of prosthetics required to play some of the alien characters. The producers therefore used the same group of people (as many as 12) in various mid-level speaking roles, taking full head and body casts from each. The group came to be unofficially known by the production as the "Babylon 5 Alien Rep Group."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Having worked on a number of television science fiction shows which had regularly gone over budget, creator J. Michael Straczynski concluded that a lack of long-term planning was to blame, and set about looking at ways in which a series could be done responsibly. Taking note of the lessons of mainstream television, which brought stories to a centralized location such as a hospital, police station, or law office, he decided that instead of "[going] in search of new worlds, building them anew each week", a fixed space station setting would keep costs at a reasonable level. A fan of sagas such as the <a href="/wiki/Foundation_series" title="Foundation series"><i>Foundation</i> series</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Childhood%27s_End" title="Childhood's End">Childhood's End</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings" title="The Lord of the Rings">The Lord of the Rings</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dune_(franchise)" title="Dune (franchise)">Dune</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Lensman_series" title="Lensman series"><i>Lensman</i> series</a>, Straczynski wondered why no one had done a television series with the same epic sweep, and concurrently with the first idea started developing the concept for a vastly ambitious epic covering massive battles and other universe-changing events. Realizing that both the fixed-locale series and the epic could be done in a single series, he began to sketch the initial outline of what would become <i>Babylon 5</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GEnie-1992_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-GEnie-1992-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-concept1_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-concept1-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:40%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>Once I had the locale, I began to populate it with characters, and sketch out directions that might be interesting. I dragged out my notes on religion, philosophy, history, sociology, psychology, science (the ones that didn't make my head explode), and started stitching together a crazy quilt pattern that eventually formed a picture. Once I had that picture in my head, once I knew what the major theme was, the rest fell into place. All at once, I saw the full five-year story in a flash, and I frantically began scribbling down notes.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">—J. Michael Straczynski, 1995<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-concept1_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-concept1-55">[55]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Straczynski set five goals for <i>Babylon 5</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> He said that the show "would have to be good science fiction" as well as good television – "rarely are shows both good [science fiction] <i>and</i> good TV; there're  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] generally one or the other [emphasis in original]." It would have to do for science fiction television what <i><a href="/wiki/Hill_Street_Blues" title="Hill Street Blues">Hill Street Blues</a></i> had done for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Police_drama" title="Police drama">police dramas</a>, by taking an adult approach to the subject. It would have to be reasonably budgeted, and "it would have to look unlike anything ever seen before on TV, presenting individual stories against a much broader canvas." He further stressed that his approach was "to take [science fiction] seriously, to build characters for grown-ups, to incorporate real science but keep the characters at the center of the story."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GEnie-1991_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-GEnie-1991-57">[57]</a></sup> Some of the staples of television science fiction were also out of the question (the show would have "no kids or cute robots").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cuterobot1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-cuterobot1-58">[58]</a></sup> The idea was not to present a perfect utopian future, but one with greed and homelessness; one where characters grow, develop, live, and die; one where not everything was the same at the end of the day's events. Citing <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a> as an influence, Straczynski said he wanted the show to be a mirror to the real world and to covertly teach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GEnie-1992_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-GEnie-1992-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Format">Format</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Format">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Described as a "window on the future" by series production designer John Iacovelli,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iacovelli1_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iacovelli1-59">[59]</a></sup> the story is set in the 23rd century on a large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Island_Three" title="Island Three">O'Neill Colony</a> named "Babylon 5"—a five-mile-long, 2.5 million-ton rotating colony designed as a gathering place for the sentient species of the galaxy, in order to foster peace through diplomacy, trade, and cooperation. Instead, acting as a center of political intrigue and conflict, the station becomes the <a href="/wiki/Linchpin" title="Linchpin">linchpin</a> of a massive <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_war" title="Interstellar war">interstellar war</a>. This is reflected in the opening monologue of each episode, which describes it as the "last, best hope for peace" in season one, then the "last, best hope for victory" in season three.</p>
<p>The series consists of a coherent five-year <a href="/wiki/Story_arc" title="Story arc">story arc</a> taking place over five seasons of 22 episodes each. Unlike most television shows at the time, <i>Babylon 5</i> was conceived as a "novel for television", with a defined beginning, middle, and end; in essence, each episode would be a single "chapter" of this "novel".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JMSyousay1_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-JMSyousay1-60">[60]</a></sup> Many of the tie-in novels, comic books, and short stories were also developed to play a significant <a href="/wiki/Canon_(fiction)" title="Canon (fiction)">canonical</a> part in the overall story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JMS1996_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-JMS1996-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cost of the series totalled an estimated $90 million for 110 episodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aicn1_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-aicn1-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">Writing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Writing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Creator and <a href="/wiki/Showrunner" title="Showrunner">showrunner</a> J. Michael Straczynski wrote 92 of the 110 episodes of <i>Babylon 5</i>, including all 44 episodes in the third and fourth seasons;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> according to Straczynski, a feat never before accomplished in American television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Other writers to have contributed scripts to the show include <a href="/wiki/Peter_David" title="Peter David">Peter David</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neil_Gaiman" title="Neil Gaiman">Neil Gaiman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kathryn_M._Drennan" title="Kathryn M. Drennan">Kathryn M. Drennan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larry_DiTillio" title="Larry DiTillio">Lawrence G. DiTillio</a>, <a href="/wiki/D._C._Fontana" title="D. C. Fontana">D. C. Fontana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/David_Gerrold" title="David Gerrold">David Gerrold</a>. <a href="/wiki/Harlan_Ellison" title="Harlan Ellison">Harlan Ellison</a>, a creative consultant on the show, received story credits for two episodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Each writer was informed of the overarching storyline, enabling the show to be produced consistently under-budget. The rules of production were strict; scripts were written six episodes in advance, and changes could not be made once production had started.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though conceived as a whole, it was necessary to adjust the plotline to accommodate external influences. Each of the characters in the series was written with a "trap door" into their background so that, in the event of an actor's unexpected departure from the series, the character could be written out with minimal impact on the storyline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In the words of Straczynski, "As a writer, doing a long-term story, it'd be dangerous and short-sighted for me to construct the story without trap doors for every single character. ... That was one of the big risks going into a long-term storyline which I considered long in advance;..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The character of <a href="/wiki/Talia_Winters" title="Talia Winters">Talia Winters</a> was to have undergone a transformation into a <a href="/wiki/Psi_Corps" title="Psi Corps">Psi Corps</a> secret agent, having been revealed as a "sleeper", whose true personality was buried subconsciously, and who acted as a spy, observing the events on the station and the actions of her command staff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> When Winters's portrayer <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Thompson" title="Andrea Thompson">Andrea Thompson</a> left the series, this revelation was used to drop the character from the series.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:40%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>First thing I did was to flip out the stand-alones, which traditionally have taken up the first 6 or so episodes of each season; between two years, that's 12 episodes, over half a season right there. Then you would usually get a fair number of additional stand-alones scattered across the course of the season. So figure another 3–4 per season, say 8, that's 20 out of 44. So now you're left with basically 24 episodes to fill out the main arc of the story.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">—Straczynski, J. Michael, 1996<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-writing_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-writing-70">[70]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Ratings for <i>Babylon 5</i> continued to rise during the show's third season, but going into the fourth season, the impending demise of network <a href="/wiki/Prime_Time_Entertainment_Network" title="Prime Time Entertainment Network">PTEN</a> left a fifth year in doubt. Unable to get word one way or the other from parent company <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a>, and unwilling to short-change the story and the fans, Straczynski began preparing modifications to the fourth season in order to allow for both eventualities. Straczynski identified three primary <a href="/wiki/Narrative_thread" title="Narrative thread">narrative threads</a> which would require resolution: the Shadow war, Earth's slide into a dictatorship, and a series of sub-threads which branched off from those. Estimating they would still take around 27 episodes to resolve without having the season feel rushed, the solution came when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turner_Network_Television" title="Turner Network Television">TNT</a> network commissioned two <i>Babylon 5</i> television films. Several hours of material was thus able to be moved into the films, including a three-episode arc which would deal with the background to the <a href="/wiki/Earth%E2%80%93Minbari_War" title="Earth–Minbari War">Earth–Minbari War</a>, and a sub-thread which would have set up the sequel series, <i>Crusade</i>. Further standalone episodes and plot-threads were dropped from season four, which could be inserted into <i>Crusade</i>, or the fifth season, were it to be given the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenlight" title="Greenlight">greenlight</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-writing_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-writing-70">[70]</a></sup> The intended series finale, "<a href="/wiki/Sleeping_in_Light" title="Sleeping in Light">Sleeping in Light</a>", was filmed during season four as a precaution against cancellation. When word came that TNT had picked up <i>Babylon 5</i>, this was moved to the end of season five and replaced with a newly filmed season four finale, "<a href="/wiki/The_Deconstruction_of_Falling_Stars" title="The Deconstruction of Falling Stars">The Deconstruction of Falling Stars</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Costume">Costume</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Costume">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ann Bruice Aling was costume designer for the show, after <a href="/wiki/Production_designer" title="Production designer">production designer</a> John Iacovelli suggested her for the position having previously worked with Bruice on a number of film and theatrical productions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iacovelli3_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iacovelli3-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the variety of costumes required she compared <i>Babylon 5</i> to "eclectic theatre", with fewer rules about period, line, shape and textures having to be adhered to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruice2_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruice2-73">[73]</a></sup> Preferring natural materials whenever possible, such as <a href="/wiki/Ostrich_leather" title="Ostrich leather">ostrich leather</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Narn" title="Narn">Narn</a> body armor, Bruice combined and layered fabrics as diverse as rayon and silk with <a href="/wiki/Brocade" title="Brocade">brocades</a> from the 1930s and '40s to give the clothing the appearance of having evolved within different cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruice1_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruice1-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim1_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kim1-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:40%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>Often we try to coordinate the sensibilities of the aliens. I try to work with Optic Nerve to ensure that the head meets the body in some sensible way. We talk about similar qualities, textures and colors and the flow of the total being. Truthfully, often the look of the prosthetic comes somewhat earlier and from that I have an understanding of what direction to go.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">— Ann Bruice Aling, 1995<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruice2_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bruice2-73">[73]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>With an interest in costume history, she initially worked closely with J. Michael Straczinski to get a sense of the historical perspective of the major alien races, "so I knew if they were a peaceful people or a warring people, cold climate etc. and then I would interpret what kind of sensibility that called for."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruice1_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bruice1-74">[74]</a></sup> Collaborating with other departments to establish co-ordinated visual themes for each race, a broad palette of colors was developed with Iacovelli, which he referred to as "spicy brights".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iacovelli1_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iacovelli1-59">[59]</a></sup> These warm shades of grey and secondary colors, such as certain blues for the <a href="/wiki/Minbari" title="Minbari">Minbari</a>, would often be included when designing both the costumes and relevant sets. As the main characters evolved, Bruice referred back to Straczynski and producer John Copeland who she viewed as "surprisingly more accessible to me as advisors than other producers and directors", so the costumes could reflect these changes. Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Londo_Mollari" title="Londo Mollari">Londo Mollari</a>'s purple coat became dark blue and more tailored while his waistcoats became less patterned and brightly colored as Bruice felt "Londo has evolved in my mind from a buffoonish character to one who has become more serious and darker."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruice2_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bruice2-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Normally there were three changes of costume for the primary actors; one for on set, one for the stunt double and one on standby in case of "coffee spills". For human civilians, garments were generally purchased <a href="/wiki/Ready-to-wear" title="Ready-to-wear">off-the-rack</a> and altered in various ways, such as removing lapels from jackets and shirts while rearranging <a href="/wiki/Hook-and-eye_closure" title="Hook-and-eye closure">closures</a>, to suggest future fashions. For some of the main female characters a more <a href="/wiki/Haute_couture" title="Haute couture">couture</a> approach was taken, as in the suits worn by <a href="/wiki/Talia_Winters" title="Talia Winters">Talia Winters</a> which Bruice described as being designed and fitted to within "an inch of their life". Costumes for the destitute residents of downbelow would be distressed through a combination of bleaching, sanding, dipping in dye baths and having stage blood added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruice1_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bruice1-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like many of the crew on the show, members of the costume department made onscreen <a href="/wiki/Cameo_appearance" title="Cameo appearance">cameos</a>. During the season 4 episode "<a href="/wiki/Atonement_(Babylon_5)" title="Atonement (Babylon 5)">Atonement</a>", the tailors and costume supervisor appeared as the Minbari women fitting <a href="/wiki/Zack_Allan" title="Zack Allan">Zack Allan</a> for his new uniform as the recently promoted head of security. His complaints, and the subsequent stabbing of him with a needle by costume supervisor Kim Holly, was a light hearted reference to the previous security uniforms, a design carried over from the pilot movie which were difficult to work with and wear due to the combination of leather and wool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim1_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kim1-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prosthetic_makeup_and_animatronics">Prosthetic makeup and animatronics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Prosthetic makeup and animatronics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While the original pilot film featured some <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">aliens</a> which were puppets and <a href="/wiki/Animatronics" title="Animatronics">animatronics</a>, the decision was made early on in the show's production to portray most alien species as <a href="/wiki/Humanoid" title="Humanoid">humanoid</a> in appearance. Barring isolated appearances, fully <a href="/wiki/Computer-generated_imagery" title="Computer-generated imagery">computer-generated</a> aliens were discounted as an idea due to the "massive <a href="/wiki/Rendering_(computer_graphics)" title="Rendering (computer graphics)">rendering</a> power"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JMSemotion_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-JMSemotion-76">[76]</a></sup> required. Long-term use of puppets and animatronics was also discounted due to the technological limitations in providing convincing interaction with the human actors ("...if you want any real <i>emotion</i> from the character, you're going to have to have an actor inside").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JMSemotion_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-JMSemotion-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visuals">Visuals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Visuals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="#Mastering_problems">§ Mastering problems</a></div>
<p>In anticipation of future <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">HDTV</a> broadcasts and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laserdisc" title="Laserdisc">Laserdisc</a> releases, rather than the usual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/4:3" title="4:3">4:3</a> format, the series was shot in <a href="/wiki/16:9" title="16:9">16:9</a>, with the image cropped to 4:3 for initial television transmissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <i>Babylon 5</i> also distinguished itself at a time when models and miniature were still standard by becoming one of the first television shows to use computer technology in creating <a href="/wiki/Visual_effects" title="Visual effects">visual effects</a>. This was achieved using <a href="/wiki/Amiga" title="Amiga">Amiga</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Video_Toaster" title="Video Toaster">Video Toasters</a> at first, and later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentium_compatible_processor" title="Pentium compatible processor">Pentium</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apple_Macintosh" title="Apple Macintosh">Macs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/DEC_Alpha" title="DEC Alpha">Alpha</a>-based systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> It also attempted to respect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newtonian_physics" title="Newtonian physics">Newtonian physics</a> in its effects sequences, with particular emphasis on the effects of inertia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Foundation_Imaging" title="Foundation Imaging">Foundation Imaging</a> provided the special effects for the pilot film (for which it won an Emmy) and the first three seasons of the show, led by Ron Thornton. After the show's co-executive producer (Douglas Netter) and producer (John Copeland) approached Straczynski with the idea of producing the effects in-house, Straczynski agreed to replace Foundation, for season 4 and 5, once a new team had been established by <a href="/wiki/Netter_Digital" title="Netter Digital">Netter Digital</a>, and an equal level of quality was assured,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> by using similar technology and a number of former Foundation employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The Emmy-winning alien make-up was provided by Optic Nerve Studios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>

src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/11px-Star_empty.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/17px-Star_empty.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/22px-Star_empty.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusic1_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusic1-83">[83]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christopher_Franke" title="Christopher Franke">Christopher Franke</a> <a href="/wiki/Musical_composition" title="Musical composition">composed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sheet_music" title="Sheet music">scored</a> the musical soundtrack for all 5 years of the show when <a href="/wiki/Stewart_Copeland" title="Stewart Copeland">Stewart Copeland</a>, who worked on the original telefilm, was unable to return for the first season due to recording and touring commitments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Given creative freedom by the producers, Franke also <a href="/wiki/Orchestration#Film_orchestration" title="Orchestration">orchestrated</a> and <a href="/wiki/Audio_mixing_(recorded_music)" title="Audio mixing (recorded music)">mixed</a> all the music which one reviewer described as having "added another dimension of mystery, suspense, and excitement to the show, with an easily distinguishable character that separates "Babylon 5" from other sci-fi television entries of the era."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChrisFranke1_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChrisFranke1-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>With his recording studio in the same building as his home located in the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Hills" title="Hollywood Hills">Hollywood Hills</a>, Franke would attend creative meetings before scoring the on average 25 minutes of music for each episode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChrisFranke1_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChrisFranke1-85">[85]</a></sup> Utilising <a href="/wiki/Steinberg_Cubase" title="Steinberg Cubase">Cubase</a> software through an <a href="/wiki/Electronic_keyboard" title="Electronic keyboard">electronic keyboard</a>, or for more complex pieces a <a href="/wiki/Light_pen" title="Light pen">light pen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graphics_tablet" title="Graphics tablet">graphics tablet</a>, he would begin by developing the <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">melodic</a> content round which the <a href="/wiki/Ambient_music" title="Ambient music">ambient</a> components and <a href="/wiki/Transition_(music)" title="Transition (music)">transitions</a> were added. Using playbacks with digital <a href="/wiki/Sampling_(music)" title="Sampling (music)">samples</a> of the appropriate instruments, such as a group of violins, he would decide which tracks to produce electronically or record <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_music" title="Acoustic music">acoustically</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChrisFranke1_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-ChrisFranke1-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChrisFranke2_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChrisFranke2-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Utilizing the "acoustic dirt produced by live instruments and the ability to play so well between two semitones" and the "frequency range, dynamics and control" provided by synthesizers, he described his approach "as experimental friendly as possible without leaving the happy marriage between the orchestral and electronic sounds".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChrisFranke2_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChrisFranke2-87">[87]</a></sup> While highlighting <i>Babylon 5</i> was produced on a "veritable shoestring", and as such would have been unable to afford a full orchestral score every week, at least one reviewer felt that the soundtrack would have benefitted from a greater use of the Berlin Symphonic Film Orchestra, which Franke established in 1991.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>And while Franke's handling of the electronic textures is adept, I think he could have made a little better use of the German orchestra that's credited in every episode. In particularly, the driving, fanfare-like title themes would gain a lot of distinction from the kind of guts that an acoustic sound would bring to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Scores for the acoustic tracks were emailed to his Berlin scoring stage, and would require from four musicians to the full orchestra, with a maximum of 24 present at any one time. One of three conductors would also be required for any score that involved more than 6 musicians. Franke would direct recording sessions via six fibre optic digital telephone lines to transmit and receive video, music and the <a href="/wiki/SMPTE_timecode" title="SMPTE timecode">SMPTE timecode</a>. The final edit and mixing of the tracks would take place in his Los Angeles studio. Initially concerned composing for an episodic television show could become "annoying because of the repetition", Franke found the evolving characters and story of <i>Babylon 5</i> afforded him the opportunity to continually "push the envelope".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChrisFranke1_85-3"><a href="#cite_note-ChrisFranke1-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChrisFranke2_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-ChrisFranke2-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Assembling music written for the series' final episode, Sleeping in Light succinctly encapsulates just how far Franke's sensibilities evolved over the course of Babylon 5's five-year production run – the synthesizer textures and martial percussion so long a fixture of the series' musical palette are now enhanced by strings and guitars that emphasize the deeply human emotional dimensions of the interstellar drama. Indeed, for all the series' obvious emphasis on fantasy and otherworldly sounds, Franke never lost track of its earthbound soul. Epic in scope and scale, Sleeping in Light boasts a power few TV soundtracks achieve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusic1_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusic1-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>A total of 24 episode and three television film soundtracks were released under Franke's record label, Sonic Images Records, between 1995 and 2001. These contain the musical scores in the same chronological order as they played in the corresponding episodes, or television films. Three compilation albums were also produced, containing extensively re-orchestrated and remixed musical passages taken from throughout the series to create more elaborate suites. In 2007 his soundtrack for <a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_The_Lost_Tales" title="Babylon 5: The Lost Tales">The Lost Tales</a> was released under the <a href="/wiki/Var%C3%A8se_Sarabande" title="Varèse Sarabande">Varèse Sarabande</a> record label.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Star_Trek:_Deep_Space_Nine_controversy"><i>Star Trek: Deep Space Nine</i> controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Star Trek: Deep Space Nine controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The pilot episode of <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_Deep_Space_Nine" title="Star Trek: Deep Space Nine">Star Trek: Deep Space Nine</a></i> (<i>DS9</i>) aired just weeks before <i>Babylon 5</i> debuted. <i>Babylon 5</i> creator J. Michael Straczynski indicated that <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Television" title="Paramount Television">Paramount Television</a> was aware of his concept as early as 1989,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> when he attempted to sell the show to the studio, and provided them with the series <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bible_(writing)" title="Bible (writing)">bible</a>, pilot script, artwork, lengthy character background histories, and plot synopses for 22 "or so planned episodes taken from the overall course of the planned series".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paramount declined to produce <i>Babylon 5</i>, but later announced <i>Deep Space Nine</i> was in development, two months after Warner Bros. announced its plans for <i>Babylon 5</i>. Straczynski stated that, even though he was confident that <i>Deep Space Nine</i> producer/creators <a href="/wiki/Rick_Berman" title="Rick Berman">Rick Berman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Piller" title="Michael Piller">Michael Piller</a> had not seen this material, he suspected that Paramount executives used his bible and scripts to steer development of <i>Deep Space Nine</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GEnie_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-GEnie-94">[94]</a></sup> He and Warner did not file suit against Paramount, largely because Straczynski didn't see it as a productive option, with negative repercussions for both TV series. In 1993 he responded to a <i>Deep Space Nine</i> fan who saw the lack of legal action as proof that Straczynski's allegation was unfounded, "If there is any (to use your term) winking and nudging going on, it's on the level of 'Okay, YOU (Paramount) know what happened, and *I* know what happened, but let's try to be grownup about it for now,' though I must say that the shapechanging thing nearly tipped me back over the edge again. If there are no more major similarities that crop up in the next few weeks or months, with luck we can continue that way."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GEnie_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-GEnie-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Babylon 5'</i>s first-run syndicated ratings averaged between 3 and 4% of US households from 1995 to 1997, whereas <i>DS9</i> ranged from 4 to 5% during the same time span.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_the_Internet">Use of the Internet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Use of the Internet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:91px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_School.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="87" data-file-width="89" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/6e/Old_School.gif" width="89"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_School.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Original Babylon 5 promo logo</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Babylon_5%27s_use_of_the_Internet" title="Babylon 5's use of the Internet">Babylon 5's use of the Internet</a></div>
<p>The show employed Internet marketing to create a buzz among online readers far in advance of the airing of the pilot episode,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> with Straczynski participating in online communities on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USENET" title="USENET">USENET</a> (in the <a href="/wiki/Rec.arts.sf.tv.babylon5.moderated" title="Rec.arts.sf.tv.babylon5.moderated">rec.arts.sf.tv.babylon5.moderated</a> newsgroup), and the <a href="/wiki/GEnie" title="GEnie">GEnie</a> and <a href="/wiki/CompuServe" title="CompuServe">CompuServe</a> systems before the Web came together as it exists today. The station's location, in "grid epsilon" at coordinates of 470/18/22, was a reference to <a href="/wiki/GEnie" title="GEnie">GEnie</a> ("grid epsilon" = "GE") and the original forum's address on the system's bulletin boards (page 470, category 18, topic 22). Also during this time, <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> executive Jim Moloshok created and distributed electronic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trading_cards" title="Trading cards">trading cards</a> to help advertise the series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> In 1995, Warner Bros. started the Official <i>Babylon 5</i> Website on the now defunct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathfinder.com" title="Pathfinder.com">Pathfinder</a> portal. In September 1995, they hired a fan to take over the site and move it to its own domain name, and to oversee the <i>Keyword B5</i> area on <a href="/wiki/AOL" title="AOL">America Online</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Broadcast_history">Broadcast history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Broadcast history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The pilot film, <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_The_Gathering" title="Babylon 5: The Gathering">The Gathering</a></i>, premiered on February 22, 1993, and the regular series initially aired from January 26, 1994 through November 25, 1998,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> first on the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Prime_Time_Entertainment_Network" title="Prime Time Entertainment Network">Prime Time Entertainment Network</a>, then in <a href="/wiki/Broadcast_syndication#First-run_syndication_in_the_U.S." title="Broadcast syndication">first-run syndication</a>. The fifth season aired on cable network <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turner_Network_Television" title="Turner Network Television">TNT</a>. The show aired every week in the United Kingdom on <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">Channel 4</a> without a break; as a result the last four or five episodes of the early seasons were shown in the UK before the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The pilot film debuted in the United States with strong viewing figures, achieving a 9.7 in the <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_ratings" title="Nielsen ratings">Nielsen</a> national syndication rankings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-benson-99">[99]</a></sup> The series proper debuted with a 6.8 rating/10 share. Figures dipped in its second week, and while it posted a solid 5.0 rating/8 share, with an increase in several major markets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> ratings for the first season continued to fall, to a low of 3.4 during <a href="/wiki/Rerun" title="Rerun">reruns</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> and then increasing again when new episodes were broadcast in July.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Ratings continued to remain low-to-middling throughout the first four seasons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> but <i>Babylon 5</i> scored well with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographics" title="Demographics">demographics</a> required to attract the leading national sponsors and saved up to $300,000 per episode by shooting off the studio lot,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-benson-99">[99]</a></sup> therefore remaining profitable for the network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The fifth season, shown on cable network TNT, had ratings about 1.0% lower than seasons two through four.</p>
<p>In the United Kingdom, <i>Babylon 5</i> was one of the better-rated US television shows on Channel 4,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> and achieved high audience <a href="/wiki/Appreciation_Index" title="Appreciation Index">Appreciation Indexes</a>, with season 4's "<a href="/wiki/Endgame_(Babylon_5)" title="Endgame (Babylon 5)">Endgame</a>" achieving the rare feat of beating the prime-time soap operas for first position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 25, 1998, after five seasons and 109 aired episodes, <i>Babylon 5</i> successfully completed its five-year story arc when TNT aired the 110th (<a href="/wiki/Epilogue" title="Epilogue">epilogue</a>) episode "<a href="/wiki/Sleeping_in_Light" title="Sleeping in Light">Sleeping in Light</a>".</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards">Awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Awards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Awards presented to <i>Babylon 5</i> include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Award" title="Primetime Emmy Award">Emmy Award</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Award_for_Outstanding_Special_Visual_Effects" title="Primetime Emmy Award for Outstanding Special Visual Effects">Outstanding Individual Achievement in Special Visual Effects</a></i>, 1993 (<i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_The_Gathering" title="Babylon 5: The Gathering">The Gathering</a></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emmy_Awards_.26_Nominations_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emmy_Awards_.26_Nominations-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li>Emmy Award: <i>Outstanding Individual Achievement in Hairstyling for a Series</i> , 1994 (episode, "<a href="/wiki/The_Parliament_of_Dreams" title="The Parliament of Dreams">The Parliament of Dreams</a>")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emmy_Awards_.26_Nominations_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emmy_Awards_.26_Nominations-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award" title="Hugo Award">Hugo Award</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award_for_Best_Dramatic_Presentation" title="Hugo Award for Best Dramatic Presentation">Best Dramatic Presentation</a></i>, 1996 (episode, "<a href="/wiki/The_Coming_of_Shadows" title="The Coming of Shadows">The Coming of Shadows</a>")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hugo1_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hugo1-6">[6]</a></sup></li>
<li>Hugo Award: <i>Best Dramatic Presentation</i>, 1997 (episode, "<a href="/wiki/Severed_Dreams" title="Severed Dreams">Severed Dreams</a>")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hugo2_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hugo2-7">[7]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Saturn_Award" title="Saturn Award">Saturn Award</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Saturn_Award_for_Best_Syndicated/Cable_Television_Series" title="Saturn Award for Best Syndicated/Cable Television Series">Best Syndicated/Cable Television Series</a></i>, 1998<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>Space Frontier Foundation Award: <i>Vision of the Future</i>, 1994</li>
<li>Space Frontier Foundation Award: <i>Vision of the Future</i>, 1996</li>
<li>E Pluribus Unum Award: (Presented By American Cinema Association), 1997<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Nominated Awards include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Emmy Award: <i>Outstanding Individual Achievement in Makeup for a Series</i>, 1995 (episode, "<a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Sacrifice" title="Acts of Sacrifice">Acts of Sacrifice</a>")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emmy_Awards_.26_Nominations_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emmy_Awards_.26_Nominations-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li>Emmy Award: <i>Outstanding Individual Achievement in Hairstyling for a Series</i>, 1995 (episode, "<a href="/wiki/The_Geometry_of_Shadows" title="The Geometry of Shadows">The Geometry of Shadows</a>")</li>
<li>Emmy Award: <i>Outstanding Individual Achievement in Cinematography for a Series</i>, 1995 (episode, "The Geometry Of Shadows")</li>
<li>Emmy Award: <i>Outstanding Cinematography for a Series</i>, 1996</li>
<li>Emmy Award: <i>Outstanding Makeup for a Series</i>, 1997 (episode, "<a href="/wiki/The_Summoning_(Babylon_5)" title="The Summoning (Babylon 5)">The Summoning</a>")</li>
<li>Emmy Award: <i>Outstanding Makeup for a Series</i>, 1998 (television movie, <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_In_the_Beginning" title="Babylon 5: In the Beginning">In The Beginning</a></i>)</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Babylon_5_media_franchise">Babylon 5 media franchise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Babylon 5 media franchise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Babylon_5_media_franchise" title="Babylon 5 media franchise">Babylon 5 media franchise</a></div>
<p>In November 1994, <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">DC</a> began publishing monthly <i>Babylon 5</i> comics. A number of short stories and novels were also produced between 1995 and 2001. Additional books were published by the gaming companies Chameleon Eclectic and <a href="/wiki/Mongoose_Publishing" title="Mongoose Publishing">Mongoose Publishing</a>, to support their desk-top strategy and <a href="/wiki/Role-playing_game" title="Role-playing game">role-playing</a> games.</p>
<p>Three telefilms were released by <a href="/wiki/TNT_(TV_channel)" title="TNT (TV channel)">Turner Network Television</a> (TNT) in 1998, after funding a fifth season of <i>Babylon 5</i>, following the demise of the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Time_Entertainment_Network" title="Prime Time Entertainment Network">Prime Time Entertainment Network</a> the previous year. In addition to <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_In_the_Beginning" title="Babylon 5: In the Beginning">In the Beginning</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_Thirdspace" title="Babylon 5: Thirdspace">Thirdspace</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_The_River_of_Souls" title="Babylon 5: The River of Souls">The River of Souls</a></i>, they released a re-edited special edition of the original 1993 telefilm, <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_The_Gathering" title="Babylon 5: The Gathering">The Gathering</a></i>. In 1999, a fifth telefilm was also produced, <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_A_Call_to_Arms" title="Babylon 5: A Call to Arms">A Call to Arms</a></i>, which acted as a pilot movie for the spin-off series <i><a href="/wiki/Crusade_(TV_series)" title="Crusade (TV series)">Crusade</a></i>, which TNT cancelled after 13 episodes had been filmed.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dell_Publishing" title="Dell Publishing">Dell Publishing</a> started publication of a series of <i>Babylon 5</i> novels in 1995, which were ostensibly considered canon within the TV series' continuity, nominally supervised by creator J. Michael Straczynski, with later novels in the line being more directly based upon Straczynski's own notes and story outlines. In 1997, <a href="/wiki/Del_Rey_Books" title="Del Rey Books">Del Rey</a> obtained the publication license from Warner Bros., and proceeded to release a number of original trilogies directly scenarized by Straczynski, as well as novelizations of three of the TNT telefilms (<i>In the Beginning, Thirdspace</i>, and <i>A Call to Arms</i>). All of the Del Rey novels are considered completely canonical within the filmic <i>Babylon 5</i> universe.</p>
<p>In 2000, the <a href="/wiki/Syfy_Universal" title="Syfy Universal">Sci-Fi Channel</a> purchased the rights to rerun the <i>Babylon 5</i> series, and premiered a new telefilm, <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_The_Legend_of_the_Rangers" title="Babylon 5: The Legend of the Rangers">The Legend of the Rangers</a></i> in 2002, which failed to be picked up as a series. In 2007, the first in a planned anthology of straight-to-DVD short stories entitled, <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_The_Lost_Tales" title="Babylon 5: The Lost Tales">The Lost Tales</a></i>, was released by <a href="/wiki/Warner_Home_Video" title="Warner Home Video">Warner Home Video</a>, but no others were produced, due to funding issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the 2014 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Diego_Comic_Con" title="San Diego Comic Con">San Diego Comic Con</a>, Straczynski announced that a Babylon 5 movie is planned to go into production in 2016. It is to be a reboot of the story, but potentially one using old cast members in different roles. <a href="/wiki/Studio_JMS" title="Studio JMS">Studio JMS</a> would produce it on a budget of $80–100 million if <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> do not take up the offer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Home_video_releases">Home video releases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Home video releases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In July 1995, <a href="/wiki/Warner_Home_Video" title="Warner Home Video">Warner Home Video</a> began distributing <i>Babylon 5</i> VHS video tapes under its Beyond Vision label in the UK. Beginning with the original telefilm, <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_The_Gathering" title="Babylon 5: The Gathering">The Gathering</a></i>, these were <a href="/wiki/PAL" title="PAL">PAL</a> tapes, showing video in the same <a href="/wiki/Aspect_ratio_(image)#Current_video_standards" title="Aspect ratio (image)">4:3 aspect ratio</a> as the initial television broadcasts. By the release of Season 2, tapes included <a href="/wiki/Closed_captioning" title="Closed captioning">closed captioning</a> of dialogue and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dolby_Surround" title="Dolby Surround">Dolby Surround</a> sound. <a href="/wiki/Columbia_House" title="Columbia House">Columbia House</a> began distributing <a href="/wiki/NTSC" title="NTSC">NTSC</a> tapes, via mail order in 1997, followed by repackaged collector's editions and three-tape <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Box_sets" title="Box sets">boxed sets</a> in 1999, by which time the original pilot telefilm had been replaced by the re-edited TNT special edition. Additional movie and complete season boxed-sets were also released by Warner Bros. until 2000.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Image_Entertainment" title="Image Entertainment">Image Entertainment</a> released <i>Babylon 5</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laserdiscs" title="Laserdiscs">laserdiscs</a> between December 1998 and September 1999. Produced on double-sided 12-inch <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Corporation" title="Pioneer Corporation">Pioneer</a> discs, each contained two episodes displayed in the 4:3 broadcast aspect-ratio, with Dolby Surround audio and closed captioning for the dialogue. Starting with two TNT telefilms, <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_In_the_Beginning" title="Babylon 5: In the Beginning">In the Beginning</a></i> and the re-edited special edition of <i>The Gathering</i>, Seasons 1 and 5 were released simultaneously over a six-month period. Seasons 2 and 4 followed, but with the decision to halt production due to a drop in sales, precipitated by rumors of a pending DVD release, only the first twelve episodes of Season 2 and the first six episodes of Season 4 were ultimately released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2001, Warner Home Video began distributing <i>Babylon 5</i> DVDs with a two-movie set containing the re-edited TNT special edition of <i>The Gathering</i> and <i>In The Beginning</i>. The telefilms were later individually released in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Region_2" title="Region 2">region 2</a> in April 2002, though some markets received the original version of <i>The Gathering</i> in identical packaging.</p>
<p>DVD boxed sets of the individual seasons, each containing six discs, began being released in October 2002. Each included a printed booklet containing episode summaries, with each disc containing audio options for German, French, and English, plus <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Special:Search/subtitle" title="wikt:Special:Search/subtitle">subtitles</a> in a wider range of languages, including Arabic and Dutch. Video was displayed in <a href="/wiki/Anamorphic_widescreen" title="Anamorphic widescreen">anamorphic widescreen</a> with <a href="/wiki/Dolby_Digital" title="Dolby Digital">Dolby Digital</a> <a href="/wiki/5.1_surround_sound" title="5.1 surround sound">5.1</a> sound. Disc 1 of each set contained an introduction to the season by <a href="/wiki/J._Michael_Straczynski" title="J. Michael Straczynski">J. Michael Straczynski</a>, while disc 6 included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Featurettes" title="Featurettes">featurettes</a> containing interviews with various production staff, as well as information on the <a href="/wiki/Fictional_universe" title="Fictional universe">fictional universe</a>, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gag_reel" title="Gag reel">gag reel</a>. Three episodes in each season also contained commentary from either Straczynski, members of the main cast, and/or the episode director.</p>
<p>Since its initial release, a number of repackaged DVD boxed sets have been produced for various <a href="/wiki/DVD_region_code" title="DVD region code">regional markets</a>. With slightly altered <a href="/wiki/Cover_art" title="Cover art">cover art</a>, they included no additional content, but the discs were more securely stored in slimline cases, rather than the early "book" format, with hard plastic pages used during the original release of the first three seasons.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_releases">Other releases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Other releases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Seasons 1 and 2, and parts of Season 3, of <i>Babylon 5</i> have been released as advertisement-supported downloads through the <a href="/wiki/In2TV" title="In2TV">In2TV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hulu" title="Hulu">Hulu</a> download services. Additionally, every episode from Seasons 1 to 5, as well as the pilot film <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5:_The_Gathering" title="Babylon 5: The Gathering">Babylon 5: The Gathering</a></i>, are available for purchase on the Xbox Live Marketplace in the United States. All five seasons, and five of the movies (<i>In The Beginning</i>, <i>Thirdspace</i>, <i>River of Souls</i>, <i>A Call To Arms</i>, <i>Legend of the Rangers</i>) are currently available through <a href="/wiki/ITunes" title="ITunes">iTunes</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mastering_problems">Mastering problems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Mastering problems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While the series was in pre-production, studios were looking at ways for their existing shows to make the transition from the then-standard <a href="/wiki/Aspect_ratio_(image)#4:3_standard" title="Aspect ratio (image)">4:3 aspect ratio</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Widescreen" title="Widescreen">widescreen</a> formats that would accompany the next generation of televisions. After visiting <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a>, who were stretching the <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_blanking_interval" title="Horizontal blanking interval">horizontal interval</a> for an episode of <i><a href="/wiki/Lois_%26_Clark:_The_New_Adventures_of_Superman" title="Lois &amp; Clark: The New Adventures of Superman">Lois &amp; Clark</a></i>, producer John Copeland convinced them to allow <i>Babylon 5</i> to be shot on <a href="/wiki/Super_35" title="Super 35">Super 35mm film stock</a>. "The idea being that we would <a href="/wiki/Telecine" title="Telecine">telecine</a> to 4:3 for the original broadcast of the series. But what it also gave us was a negative that had been shot for the new 16×9 widescreen-format televisions that we knew were on the horizon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copeland74_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Copeland74-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:40%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>The widescreen conversion thing was executive short sightedness at its finest!!! We offered to do ALL of <i>Babylon 5</i> in widescreen mode if Warner Bros would buy us a reference monitor so we could check our output. (only $5000 at the time) Ken Parkes (the "Business affairs" guy) and Netter (penny wise, but pound foolish) said no! So we did everything so it could be CROPPED to be widescreen! Each blamed the other by the way. Doug Netter said, "Ken Parkes said no". Ken Parkes said, "Doug Netter said no". SHEESH!!! So for $75 an episode they could have had AWESOME near Hi-Def.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">— Ron Thornton, 2008<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Though the CGI scenes, and those containing live action combined with digital elements, could have been created in a suitable widescreen format, a cost-saving decision was taken to produce them in the 4:3 aspect ratio. The intention was to then <a href="/wiki/Cropping_(image)" title="Cropping (image)">crop</a> the top and bottom of the images, and <a href="/wiki/Video_scaler" title="Video scaler">upscale</a> the resolution for any future widescreen release or broadcast. In 2000, when the show was transferred to widescreen for airing on the <a href="/wiki/Syfy" title="Syfy">Sci-Fi Channel</a> prior to its eventual DVD release, the plan was not followed, as John Copeland recalls: "They did another video hack, and simply used a digital post production device like a DVE (Digital Video Effects) to blow the material up. They essentially stretched it approximately 1/3 to fill the larger aspect ratio."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copeland74_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Copeland74-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>The scenes containing live action ready to be <a href="/wiki/Digital_compositing" title="Digital compositing">composited</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matte_paintings" title="Matte paintings">matte paintings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_animation" title="Computer animation">CGI animation</a>, etc., were delivered on tape already telecined to the 4:3 aspect-ratio, and contained a high level of <a href="/wiki/Film_grain" title="Film grain">grain</a>, which resulted in further <a href="/wiki/Image_noise" title="Image noise">image noise</a> being present when enlarged and stretched for widescreen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> For the purely live-action scenes, rather than using the film negatives, "Warners had even forgotten that they had those. They used <a href="/wiki/PAL" title="PAL">PAL</a> versions and converted them to <a href="/wiki/NTSC" title="NTSC">NTSC</a> for the US market. They actually didn't go back and retransfer the shows."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copeland75_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Copeland75-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the resulting <a href="/wiki/Aliasing" title="Aliasing">aliasing</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_scan" title="Progressive scan">progressive scan</a> transfer of the video to DVD, this has created a number of visual flaws throughout the widescreen release. In particular, quality has been noted to drop significantly in composite shots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Babylon_5&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
